                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of the progenitor of wheat A subgenome Triticum urartu"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2018 557:7705"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-05-09"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 Macmillan Publishers Ltd., part of Springer Nature"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Triticum urartu (diploid, AA) is the progenitor of the A subgenome of tetraploid (Triticum turgidum, AABB) and hexaploid (Triticum aestivum, AABBDD) wheat1,2. Genomic studies of T. urartu have been useful for investigating the structure, function and evolution of polyploid wheat genomes. Here we report the generation of a high-quality genome sequence of T. urartu by combining bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC)-by-BAC sequencing, single molecule real-time whole-genome shotgun sequencing3, linked reads and optical mapping4,5. We assembled seven chromosome-scale pseudomolecules and identified protein-coding genes, and we suggest a model for the evolution of T. urartu chromosomes. Comparative analyses with genomes of other grasses showed gene loss and amplification in the numbers of transposable elements in the T. urartu genome. Population genomics analysis of 147&#160;T. urartu accessions from across the Fertile Crescent showed clustering of three groups, with differences in altitude and biostress, such as powdery mildew disease. The T. urartu genome assembly provides a valuable resource for studying genetic variation in wheat and related grasses, and promises to facilitate the discovery of genes that could be useful for wheat improvement. The genome sequence of Triticum urartu, the progenitor of the A subgenome of hexaploid wheat, provides insight into genome duplication during grass evolution."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Triticum urartu</i> (diploid, AA) is the progenitor of the A subgenome of tetraploid (<i>Triticum turgidum</i>, AABB) and hexaploid (<i>Triticum aestivum</i>, AABBDD) wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="DvorÃ¡k, J., Terlizzi, P., Zhang, H. B. &amp; Resta, P. The evolution of polyploid wheats: identification of the A genome donor species. Genome 36, 21â31 (1993)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d28976e762">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Peng, J. H., Sun, D. H. &amp; Nevo, E. Domestication evolution, genetics and genomics in wheat. Mol. Breed. 28, 281â301 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28976e765">2</a></sup>. Genomic studies of <i>T. urartu</i> have been useful for investigating the structure, function and evolution of polyploid wheat genomes. Here we report the generation of a high-quality genome sequence of <i>T. urartu</i> by combining bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC)-by-BAC sequencing, single molecule real-time whole-genome shotgun sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ferrarini, M. et al. An evaluation of the PacBio RS platform for sequencing and de novo assembly of a chloroplast genome. BMC Genomics 14, 670 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28976e776">3</a></sup>, linked reads and optical mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zheng, G. X. et al. Haplotyping germline and cancer genomes with high-throughput linked-read sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 303â311 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28976e780">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771â776 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28976e783">5</a></sup>. We assembled seven chromosome-scale pseudomolecules and identified protein-coding genes, and we suggest a model for the evolution of <i>T. urartu</i> chromosomes. Comparative analyses with genomes of other grasses showed gene loss and amplification in the numbers of transposable elements in the <i>T. urartu</i> genome. Population genomics analysis of 147Â <i>T. urartu</i> accessions from across the Fertile Crescent showed clustering of three groups, with differences in altitude and biostress, such as powdery mildew disease. The <i>T. urartu</i> genome assembly provides a valuable resource for studying genetic variation in wheat and related grasses, and promises to facilitate the discovery of genes that could be useful for wheat improvement.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genome of <i>T. urartu</i> (Tu) accession G1812 (PI428198) was sequenced and assembled (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1aâc</a>). The assembled contig sequences were 4.79âGb with an N50 (the length <i>N</i> for which 50% of all bases in the sequences are in a sequence of length <i>L</i>â&lt;âN) of 344âkb and scaffold sequencesÂ were 4.86âGb with an N50 of 3.67âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab1">1</a>), very close to the estimated genome size of 4.94âGb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ling, H.-Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28976e840">6</a></sup>. We anchored 4.67âGb (95.9%) of the scaffold sequences onto Tu chromosomes with a high density single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) genetic map (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1d</a>), generating seven DNA pseudomolecules (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM3">1</a>, Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1e</a>). The high quality of the assembled sequences was confirmed at both the nucleotide and chromosome levels by comparison with previously published BAC sequences and the draft genome sequence of <i>Triticum aestivum</i> (Ta)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28976e856">7</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig5">2</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM4">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the Tu genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We predicted 41,507 protein-coding genes, including 37,516 high-confidence and 3,991 low-confidence genes (Extended Data TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab2">1a</a>) using the Gramene pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liang, C., Mao, L., Ware, D. &amp; Stein, L. Evidence-based gene predictions in plant genomes. Genome Res. 19, 1912â1923 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1213">8</a></sup>. On average, the genes have transcript length of 1,453âbp, protein length of 332 amino acids, and 4.5 exons per transcript, which were comparable to genes in other grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1217">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1220">10</a></sup> (Extended Data TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab2">1b</a>). Approximately 88.18% of the predicted genes were assigned functional annotations (Extended Data TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab2">1c</a>). We also predicted that 10,514 genes could produce alternatively spliced transcripts, with an average of 2.95 transcripts per gene. Moreover, we identified 31,269 microRNAs (miRNAs), 5,810 long non-coding RNAs (lncRNAs), 3,620 transfer RNAs (tRNAs), 80 ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs) and 2,519 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) throughout the genome (Extended Data TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab2">1d</a>).</p><p>A total of 3.90âGb (81.42%) of genome sequences was identified as repetitive elements, including 3.44âGb (71.83%) of retrotransposons and 355âMb (7.41%) of DNA transposons (Extended Data TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab2">1e</a>). Among long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, the Gypsy and Copia superfamilies comprised 42.71% and 24.30% of the genome, respectively. Further, we identified 48,370 intact Gypsy retrotransposons for which the peak of amplification bursts appeared at more than one million years ago (Ma) and 35,559 intact Copia retrotransposons with a peak less than 1âMa (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig1">1a</a>). We also identified 121,792 solo-LTR/Gypsy and 44,349 solo-LTR/Copia elements, yielding solo-LTR/intact element ratios of 2.5 and 1.2 for Gypsy and Copia retrotransposons, respectively. These results showed an earlier burst of Gypsy retrotransposition than of Copia retrotransposition in the Tu genome; both bursts occurred after the divergence of the A and B genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Marcussen, T. et al. Ancient hybridizations among the ancestral genomes of bread wheat. Science 345, 1250092 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1243">11</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Recent LTR retrotransposon bursts in the Tu genome and distribution of genomic components on Tu chromosome 1."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Recent LTR retrotransposon bursts in the Tu genome and distribution of genomic components on Tu chromosome 1.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="935" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Insertion burst of LTR retrotransposons of Gypsy and Copia. TE, transposable element. <b>b</b>â<b>l</b>, Multi-dimensional display of genomic components of Tu chromosome 1. <b>b</b>, DNA pseudomolecule. <b>c</b>, Gene frequency (number of genes per 10âMb). <b>d</b>, Repeat density (per cent nucleotides per 5âMb). <b>e</b>, Density of LTR retrotransposons (per cent nucleotides per 10âMb). <b>f</b>, Frequency of lncRNA (log[number per 10âMb]). <b>g</b>, Frequency of segmentally duplicated genes (log[number per 1âMb]). <b>h</b>, Frequency of tandemly duplicated genes (log[number per 1âMb]). <b>i</b>, Frequency of simple sequence repeats (log[number per 10âMb]). <b>j</b>, Linkage map distance (cM per 5âMb). <b>k</b>, Accumulated gene expression level (log<sub>2</sub>[FPKM (fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads) per 5âMb]). <b>l</b>, GC content (per cent per 1âMb).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We found substantially higher gene density and recombination rates, as well as lower densities of transposable elements and tandem repeats, near the telomeres of each chromosome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig1">1bâl</a>, Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig6">3</a>). LTR retrotransposons were distributed unevenly throughout each chromosome. The distribution of Copia elements was enriched at both telomericâsubtelomeric regions, whereas Gypsy retrotransposons were enriched in the pericentromericâcentromeric regions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig1">1e</a>, Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig6">3</a>). The accumulated gene expression level was higher in subtelomeres than in the centromeric regions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig1">1k</a>, Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig6">3</a>).</p><p>Analyses of genes in the Tu genome together with those from rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1338">9</a></sup>, maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1342">12</a></sup>, sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1346">13</a></sup> and <i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1352">10</a></sup> were clustered into 24,860 gene families (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig7">4a</a>). Of these, 10,681 families were shared among the five examined plant genomes, representing a core set of genes across these grass genomes. There were 4,610 genes from 1,567 gene families that were specific to Tu, of which many have functional gene ontology annotations relating to responses to stimulus and stress (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig7">4b</a>).</p><p>By comparing transcription factors in the Tu genome with those of the six sequenced grass genomes in the iTAK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zheng, Y. et al. iTAK: A program for genome-wide prediction and classification of plant transcription factors, transcriptional regulators, and protein kinases. Mol. Plant 9, 1667â1670 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1366">14</a></sup> collection, including <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, sorghum, maize, <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> (Aet)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature 496, 91â95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1376">15</a></sup> and Ta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1380">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature 491, 705â710 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1383">16</a></sup>, we found that the number of reproductive meristem (REM) subfamily genes in the transcription factor B3 family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Swaminathan, K., Peterson, K. &amp; Jack, T. The plant B3 superfamily. Trends Plant Sci. 13, 647â655 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1388">17</a></sup> was amplified in the genomes of Tu, Aet and Ta (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM5">3</a>, Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig7">4c</a>, Supplementary Information S1.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">1</a>). The REM subfamily is functionally related to vernalization and flower development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Levy, Y. Y., Mesnage, S., Mylne, J. S., Gendall, A. R. &amp; Dean, C. Multiple roles of Arabidopsis VRN1 in vernalization and flowering time control. Science 297, 243â246 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1401">18</a></sup>. Therefore, we speculate that the amplification of B3 REM transcription factors in wheat genomes might be related to the vernalization process of wheat. Furthermore, we identified 598 disease resistance genes (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM6">4</a>) and 22 prolamin genes (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM7">5</a>, Supplementary Information S1.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>We identified three large structural variations that occurred in either Tu or Ta, with clearly defined boundaries, by comparing the Tu genome to the draft sequences of three sub-genomes of hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1418">7</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig2">2a, b</a>, Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig8">5a</a>, Supplementary Information S2.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">1</a>). We aligned the Tu genome with sequences from six BACs of the A subgenome of <i>T. turgidum</i> (Tt) and eleven BACs from the A subgenome of Ta, and found that the unaligned regions between the BAC and the Tu genomic sequences resulted from the insertion of LTR retrotransposons in either Tu or Tt and/or Ta (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig8">5b</a>). Furthermore, we compared the chromosome 7 assembly of the A subgenome of Ta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885â896 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1438">19</a></sup> (Ta7A) to Tu chromosome 7 (Tu7), and found that 655âMb (91.03%) and 536âMb (90.06%) of Tu7 and Ta7A sequences, respectively, were aligned to each other at a minimum identity of 90% or lower, with many unaligned retrotransposon regions (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig8">5c, d</a>). These results show that the different wheat A genomes experienced large-scale structural rearrangements both before and after polyploidization with other genomes, and have experienced independent gain or loss of LTR retrotransposons after the polyploidization event.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome synteny to bread wheat (Ta) and an evolutionary model of the Tu chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Genome synteny to bread wheat (Ta) and an evolutionary model of the Tu chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="917" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b>, Synteny of Tu chromosomes with subgenomes A and B of Ta. Each line represents a syntenic block of five or more gene pairs with similarity of 80% or more. Three large structural variations detected are: (1) a reciprocal translocation at the distal end of the long arms between Tu4 and Tu5 that occurred before the polyploidization of the A and B genomes, but after divergence from both B and D genomes; (2) a one-way translocation from Ta7B to Ta4A; and (3) a pericentric inversion on Ta4A involving most of the long and short arms. <b>c</b>, Evolutionary model of Tu chromosomes from an ancestral grass genome based on the AGK structure initially defined in Murat et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Murat, F. et al. Shared subgenome dominance following polyploidization explains grass genome evolutionary plasticity from a seven protochromosome ancestor with 16K protogenes. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 12â33 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1465">24</a></sup> and the syntenic relationships of Tu with <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bd), rice (Os), and sorghum (Sb). One-directional arrows indicate segment translocations, and bidirectional arrows indicate inversions. Tu1âTu7, seven chromosomes of Tu; A1âA12, twelve chromosomes of the grass ancestor; Bd1âBd5, five chromosomes of Bd; Os1âOs12, twelve rice chromosomes; Sb1âSb10, ten sorghum chromosomes. The seven coloured squares on the right represent seven basic ancient grass chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Murat, F. et al. Shared subgenome dominance following polyploidization explains grass genome evolutionary plasticity from a seven protochromosome ancestor with 16K protogenes. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 12â33 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1472">24</a></sup>. The line graphs below Tu chromosomes display the frequency distribution of AGK genes. The red and blue arrows indicate inter- and intra-chromosome fusion locations, respectively, of the ancestral chromosomes in the Tu genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Tu shared with rice, <i>Brachypodium</i> and sorghum a common grass ancestor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kellogg, E. A. Evolutionary history of the grasses. Plant Physiol. 125, 1198â1205 (2001)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1490">20</a></sup> with seven pairs of ancient chromosomes, which became 12 pairs of ancestral chromosomes (still maintained in rice) after one round of whole-genome duplication (WGD, 70âMa) and two additional chromosomal fusions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, X., Shi, X., Hao, B., Ge, S. &amp; Luo, J. Duplication and DNA segmental loss in the rice genome: implications for diploidization. New Phytol. 165, 937â946 (2005)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1494">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11â24 (2008)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1494_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Singh, N. K. et al. Single-copy genes define a conserved order between rice and wheat for understanding differences caused by duplication, deletion, and transposition of genes. Funct. Integr. Genomics 7, 17â35 (2007)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1494_2">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Murat, F. et al. Shared subgenome dominance following polyploidization explains grass genome evolutionary plasticity from a seven protochromosome ancestor with 16K protogenes. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 12â33 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1497">24</a></sup>. By studying the collinear relationships among these species (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig9">6</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM8">6</a>), we found that Tu3 and Tu6 are the most conserved Tu chromosomes, each being derived from a single ancient chromosome shared by Os1, Bd2 and Sb3 and Os2, Bd3 and Sb4, respectively (where Os is <i>Oryza sativa</i>, Bd is <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> and Sb is <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>). Chromosomes Tu1, Tu2, Tu4 and Tu7 are each composed of chromosomal segments originating from two different ancient chromosomes. Tu5, however, was derived from three ancient chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig2">2c</a>, Supplementary Information S2.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">2</a>). These results are consistent with the model proposed by Murat et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Murat, F. et al. Shared subgenome dominance following polyploidization explains grass genome evolutionary plasticity from a seven protochromosome ancestor with 16K protogenes. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 12â33 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1523">24</a></sup>, and we further narrowed the fusion boundaries to small regions of approximately hundreds of kilobases.</p><p>Using an approach based on that described by Murat et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Murat, F., Armero, A., Pont, C., Klopp, C. &amp; Salse, J. Reconstructing the genome of the most recent common ancestor of flowering plants. Nat. Genet. 49, 490â496 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1530">25</a></sup>, we inferred 11,718 Tu ancestral grass karyotype (AGK) genes, which account for 31.2% of all chromosome localized Tu genes, slightly lower than the percentage detected in rice (32.4%) and considerably lower than that in <i>Brachypodium</i> (47.4%). The AGK genes were depleted in pericentromeric and subtelomeric regions, as well as chromosomal fusion locations (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig2">2c</a>, Supplementary Information S2.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>We performed intragenomic comparisons in Tu and found five clearly visible collinear regions in the dot plots (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig10">7</a>, Supplementary Information S2.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">3</a>). These regions originated from four pairs of anciently duplicated chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, X., Shi, X., Hao, B., Ge, S. &amp; Luo, J. Duplication and DNA segmental loss in the rice genome: implications for diploidization. New Phytol. 165, 937â946 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1552">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11â24 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1555">22</a></sup>. However, compared to the rice genome, the collinearity was disrupted between each pair of the Tu chromosome segments derived from the remaining ancient chromosomes, owing to the loss of one or both copies of ancestral genes. For example, of the 2,620 anciently duplicated gene pairs still maintained in rice, approximately 47% and 38% had lost one and both copies, respectively, in the syntenic regions of Tu (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig10">7g</a>).</p><p>Comparative analysis of chromosomes Tu3 and Ta3B<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science 345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1566">26</a></sup> identified variations between the two chromosomes at both nucleotide and protein levels (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig11">8aâg</a>, Supplementary Information S2.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">4</a>). We identified 617âMb (82.6%) and 651âMb (84.1%) of syntenic sequences in Tu3 and Ta3B, respectively, with only 3,103 (52.32%) genes of Tu3 being aligned to 3,542 (52.99%) genes of Ta3B at a minimum protein identity of 50% and a minimum coverage of 50%. By comparison with syntenic genes from <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice and sorghum, we identified 393 and 213 deleted genes and 354 and 648 inserted genes within the collinear segments of Tu3 and Ta3B, respectively (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM9">7</a>), highlighting the increased number of genes in Ta3B relative to Tu3. We also found a recent LTR retrotransposon burst in Ta3B, which was not observed in Tu3 (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig11">8h</a>). Together, these differences may contribute to the larger size of Ta3B in comparison to Tu3, and suggest that amplification of LTR retrotransposons after the divergence of the A and B genomes may be relevant for wheat genome evolution.</p><p>For population genetic studies, we sequenced the leaf transcriptome of 147Â <i>T. urartu</i> accessions collected from six countries in the Fertile Crescent (Armenia, Iran, Iraq, Syria, Turkey and Lebanon) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM10">8</a>, Supplementary Information S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM1">3</a>) and identified 144,806 high-quality SNPs from 22,841 expressed genes (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig12">9a</a>). We analysed population structure using STRUCTURE, and showed that based on this or phylogenetic analyses, the Tu accessions clustered into three groups (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig3">3b, c</a>). Group I contained 30 accessions from multiple countries. Group II contained 64 accessions, 88% of which were from Lebanon. Group III contained 53 accessions, with 92% from Turkey. These groups have differences in the collection site altitudes, with the majority of Group II accessions being from altitudes above 1,000âm (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig12">9b</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM10">8</a>). The genetic diversity was lowest in Group II (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig12">9c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Geographic distribution and population structure of Tu."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Geographic distribution and population structure of Tu.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="411" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of the 147 Tu accessions at different altitudes of the Fertile Crescent. Also shown are the main land markers, including the Euphrates and Tigris rivers, Urmia lake and 11 cities (Adana (Ad), Aleppo (Al), Baghdad (Ba), Beirut (Be), Damascus (D), Erzurum (E), Homs (H), Mosul (M), Tabriz (Ta), Tripoli (Tr) and Yerevan (Y)). The map was drawn using the online mapping tool ArcGIS (version 10.1, <a href="http://www.esri.com">www.esri.com</a>). <b>b</b>, Phylogenetic clustering of the 147 accessions into three groups, with <i>B. distachyon</i> as the outgroup. <b>c</b>, Population structure analysis of Tu accessions, clustering with three groups that are similar to those from the phylogenetic analysis shown in <b>b</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>After inoculation with the powdery mildew pathogen <i>Blumeria graminis</i> f. sp. <i>tritici</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhang, J. et al. Coexpression network analysis of the genes regulated by two types of resistance responses to powdery mildew in wheat. Sci. Rep. 6, 23805 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1666">27</a></sup> (Bgt, race E09), 92.2% of the accessions in Group II exhibited resistance, whereas most of the accessions in Groups I and III (96.7% and 90.6%, respectively) were susceptible (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig12">9d</a>). We conducted genomic scans for selective sweeps, and detected 141 (<i>Ï</i><sub>Group I</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>Group II</sub>â&gt;â7.7) and 143 (<i>Ï</i><sub>Group III</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>Group II</sub>â&gt;â4.3) candidate sweep signals based on SNP diversity ratios in the compared groups (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig12">9e</a>). These regions included 239 high-confidence predicted genes (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM11">9</a>), including those with functional annotations for transcriptional regulation (32 genes), signal transduction (14) or detoxification of reactive oxygen species and stress defence (14); 23 of the genes were Tu-specific. Previous studies have found that plant and animal adaptation to high altitudes involves genes with functions in diverse physiological and molecular processes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ai, H. et al. Population history and genomic signatures for high-altitude adaptation in Tibetan pigs. BMC Genomics 15, 834 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1697">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ma, L. et al. Physiological, biochemical and proteomics analysis reveals the adaptation strategies of the alpine plant Potentilla saundersiana at altitude gradient of the Northwestern Tibetan Plateau. J. Proteomics 112, 63â82 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1700">29</a></sup>. Further analysis showed a wall-associated receptor protein kinase gene (<i>TuWAK</i>, TuG1812G0400002796) within a selective sweep signal, which had two haplotypes (Hap1 and Hap2) with differing distributions among the three groups (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig12">9f, g</a>). Hap1 was the major haplotype in Group II, and associated with resistance to Bgt. Hap2 was the main haplotype in Groups I and III, and associated with Bgt susceptibility. The maize homologue <i>ZmWAK</i> is involved in defence against fungal pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zuo, W. et al. A maize wall-associated kinase confers quantitative resistance to head smut. Nat. Genet. 47, 151â157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1713">30</a></sup>. Therefore, <i>TuWAK</i> may have been under selection in Group II accessions, and contributed to both high altitude adaptation and powdery mildew resistance.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plants</h3><p>The accession G1812 (PI428198) of <i>T. urartu</i> was previously shown by restriction fragment length polymorphism (RFLP) analysis to have the closest relationship to the A subgenome of hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Akhunov, E. D., Akhunova, A. R. &amp; DvorÃ¡k, J. BAC libraries of Triticum urartu, Aegilops speltoides and Ae. tauschii, the diploid ancestors of polyploid wheat. Theor. Appl. Genet. 111, 1617â1622 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1736">31</a></sup>. Its genome was sequenced using a whole-genome shotgun strategy on the Illumina HiSeq2000 platform, and a draft genome has been generated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ling, H.-Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1740">6</a></sup>. In this study, we also used accession G1812 of <i>T. urartu</i> to improve its genome assembly quality. We combined BAC-by-BAC sequencing with single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing technology (Pacific Biosciences), and new mapping technologies (BioNano genome map and 10Ã Genomics linked reads) to generate a high-quality reference sequence of <i>T. urartu</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec4">BAC library construction</h3><p>The genomic BAC libraries of <i>T. urartu</i> accession G1812 (PI428198) were constructed by AMPLICON Express (Pullman). In brief, nuclei were isolated from leaves of one-month-old plants, and embedded in agarose plugs. After lysis of nucleic membrane and digestion of proteins, intact high molecular weight (HMW) genomic DNA was extracted as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Zhang, H. B., Zhao, X. P., Ding, X. L., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wing, R. A. Preparation of megabase-size DNA from plant nuclei. Plant J. 7, 175â184 (1995)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1761">32</a></sup>. The HMW DNA was partially digested using EcoRI, HindIII and MboI to generate insertion DNA fragments for BAC library construction. Subsequently, the DNA fragments (average sizes 120â180âkb) were ligated into the appropriate sites on the vector pCC1BAC or pECBAC1 or pIndigoBAC-5. The ligations were transformed into <i>Escherichia coli</i> DH10B cells (phage resistant). Transformants were robotically picked and arrayed onto 384-well plates. All plates were assigned a barcode and recorded in a database. In total, 470,016 BAC clones were obtained, including 184,320 clones in the EcoRI-digest library with an average insert size of 125âkb, 193,536 clones in the HindIII-digest library with an average insert size of 110âkb, and 92,160 clones in the MboI-digest library with an average insert size of 115âkb. The total insert DNA length was about 54.9âGb, approximately 11 equivalents of the whole genome of <i>T. urartu</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec5">Whole-genome profiling of <i>T. urartu</i>
                        </h3><p>To generate BAC fingerprint contigs, 451,584 BAC clones (179,712 EcoRI clones, 179,712 HindIII clones and 92,160 MboI clones) representing about 10 equivalents of the <i>T. urartu</i> genome were analysed using the whole-genome profiling (WGP) technology by Keygene N.V.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="van Oeveren, J. et al. Sequence-based physical mapping of complex genomes by whole genome profiling. Genome Res. 21, 618â625 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1786">33</a></sup>. After pooling of individual BAC clones, isolation of pooled BAC DNA, digestion with HindIII/MseI, ligation of Illumina Hiseq adaptor sequences and sequencing from the HindIII side using Illumina HiSeq with 100-nt read length, we obtained 1,108,197 filtered WGP tags and 345,233 tagged BACs. Then, the sequence-based physical maps were assembled using an adapted version<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="van Oeveren, J. et al. Sequence-based physical mapping of complex genomes by whole genome profiling. Genome Res. 21, 618â625 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1790">33</a></sup> of the FingerPrint Contig (FPC) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Soderlund, C., Longden, I. &amp; Mott, R. FPC: a system for building contigs from restriction fingerprinted clones. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 523â535 (1997)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1794">34</a></sup> to generate a high-stringency map (using a threshold of 1.0âÃâ10<sup>â28</sup> DQ) and a reduced-stringency map, in which end-to-end merging of contigs was done and singletons were added in a number of repetitive steps (1.0âÃâ10<sup>â25</sup> DQâ+âend merges 10<sup>â15</sup> +âsingleton merges 10<sup>â15</sup>). The total length of BAC contigs reached 5.52âGb with a BAC contig N50 size of 340âkb for high-stringency WGP map assembly and 4.68âGb with a BAC contig N50 size of 656âkb for reduced-stringency WGP map assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec6">Selection of BAC clones and BAC DNA extraction</h3><p>Using WGP data, 47,200 BAC clones were selected from the tagged 345,233 BAC clones, which were assembled on 20,702 BAC contigs with high-stringency WGP assembly, according to a minimal tiling path (MTP) principle for genomic sequencing via BAC-by-BAC sequencing. The selected MTP BAC clones were divided into two groups (the two neighbouring BACs on each FPC were separated into group A and group B to avoid the misdistribution of sequence reads on BACs after sequencing).</p><p>For BAC DNA extraction, each selected BAC clone was incubated in 400âÂµl lysogeny broth (LB) with 12.5âÂµg/ml chloramphenicol on 96-well plates at 400 r.p.m. and 37âÂ°C for 16âh. After that, we pooled 2,304 individual BAC clones from 24 pieces of 96-well plates into 96 pools using 2D pooling strategy at horizontal and vertical levels (only BACs from the same group could be pooled together). Each pool contained 48 different BAC clones. For the pooling, 75âÂµl bacterial solution of each BAC clone was picked up and pooled together as described above for BAC DNA extraction. The BAC DNA was extracted using PhasePrep BAC DNA Kit (Sigma-Aldrich) following the manufacturerâs instructions with some modifications. In brief, bacterial cells were collected by centrifugation for 10âmin at 4âÂ°C, and re-suspended in 120âÂµl chilled resuspension solution containing RNase A solution (20âmg/ml). After addition of 120âÂµl lysis solution, the bacterial solution was well mixed with the lysis solution by gentle turnover four times, and put on ice for 4âmin. Subsequently, 120âÂµl pre-chilled neutralization solution was added and mixed again by gentle turnover four times. After incubation on ice for 5âmin, the lysed bacterial solution was centrifuged at 13,000 r.p.m. and 4âÂ°C for 4âmin. Then, the supernatant was transferred to a clean 1.5-ml Eppendorf tube and mixed with 250âÂµl isopropanol by gentle turnover several times. BAC DNA was pelleted by centrifugation at 13,000 r.p.m. and 4âÂ°C for 20âmin. After removal of the supernatant, the pellet was washed using 100âÂµl 70% ethanol and dried in vacuum. To remove RNAs, we added 500âÂµl elution solution and 1âÂµl tenfold diluted RNase cocktail to suspend the pellet. After incubation at 60âÂ°C for 5âmin, 20âÂµl sodium acetate buffer solution and 50âÂµl endotoxin removal solution were added separately to the DNA solution, mixed and incubated at 37âÂ°C for 5âmin. Next, the clean upper phase solution was transferred to a new Eppendorf tube and mixed with 270âÂµl DNA precipitation solution, and BAC DNA was pelleted by centrifugation at 13,000 r.p.m. and 4âÂ°C for 10âmin. Then, the DNA pellet was washed twice using 400âÂµl 70% ethanol and dried in vacuum. Finally, the BAC DNA was dissolved in 25âÂµl sterile deionized water, and kept at â20âÂ°C until use. In total, 47,223 BAC clones were pooled into 984 BAC pools and their DNAs were extracted and used for sequencing</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec7">Preparation of Illumina sequencing libraries and sequencing</h3><p>For sequencing of BACs by Illumina HiSeq2500, we constructed paired-end libraries with ~300âbp insert size following the protocol of NEBNext Ultra DNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (New England Biolabs) with a slight modification. In brief, 50âng DNA of each BAC pool (containing 48 BAC clones) was sheared in a Covaris S2 focused ultrasonicator to an average insert size of 300ânt. After end reparation with the NEBNext Ultra End Repair/dA-Tailing Module Kit (New England Biolabs), the DNA fragments were ligated with barcode adaptors, and 96 BAC DNA samples with different barcode adapters were mixed together and purified using a QIAquick PCR Purification Kit (Qiagen). Subsequently, approximately 300-bp DNA fragments were selected again on 2% agarose gel, and amplified by PCR with 11 cycles. The library was then sequenced using 150 base-length read chemistry in a paired-end flow cell on the Illumina HiSeq2500 after library profile analysis by the Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer and qPCR quantification. Paired-end sequencing was performed following the manufacturerâs protocol (<a href="http://www.illumina.com/">http://www.illumina.com/</a>) based on the workflow: cluster generation, template hybridization, isothermal amplification, linearization, blocking, denaturation and hybridization of sequencing primers. The base-calling pipeline (Hiseq2500) was used to detect bases from the raw fluorescent images. In total, 39 libraries, including 2,347 pools (48 BACs per pool) with 300âbp insert size, were prepared, 33 sequencing lanes were run in Hiseq2500 and 2,102âGb of raw sequence data was generated (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec8">Data quality control</h3><p>The raw datasets were first filtered by trimming reads with low-quality bases (qualityâ&lt;â2) at the front end and reads with quality &lt;10 at the back end, and by discarding reads with 20% low-quality bases (quality &lt;10) and reads with a length &lt;75 base pairs. Then, we removed the contaminated sequence reads by blasting the sequence reads with the genomes of <i>E. coli</i>, mitochondria, chloroplast, and the vector sequence as well human genomic sequence with BWA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1848">35</a></sup> (Burrows-Wheeler Aligner, <a href="http://bio-bwa.sourceforge.net/">http://bio-bwa.sourceforge.net/</a>). On average, 20% of reads were aligned to microbial genome; 4.5% of reads were aligned to vector genome; and 0.5% of reads were aligned to chloroplast, mitochondrion and human genomes. Finally, 75% of sequence reads with a total length of 1,471âGb (about 294Ã <i>T. urartu</i> genome) remained for assembly of the <i>T. urartu</i> genome.</p><p>To confirm that the sequence reads assigned to each BAC were from the right BAC clone, we blasted sequence reads of each BAC pool against the WGP BAC tags. BAC pools with sequence reads less than 400âMb were sequenced again to obtain enough sequences for assembly. We also found that sequence reads of a few BAC pools were blasted to two or more neighbour WGP BAC tags owing to BAC clone contamination. Thus, these BAC clones were picked again and their DNA was extracted and resequenced.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec9">Preparation of PacBio sequencing libraries and sequencing</h3><p>To enable assembly of complex repeat structure and GC- and AT-rich regions, which are often unassembled or highly fragmented in next generation sequencing (NGS)-based draft genomes, we also performed whole-genome shotgun sequencing using SMRT sequencing technology (Pacific Biosciences). The library preparation and sequencing were done by Nextomics. Sequencing libraries with 20-kb DNA inserts were prepared following the protocol of the PacBio template preparation kit (DNA Template Prep Kit 1.0) and sequenced using Pacific Biosciences RSII instrument. A total of 109 SMRT cells were processed. Subread filtering was performed using Pacific Biosciences SMRT analysis software (v2.3.1) with the parameters (subread lengthâ=â50, minimum polymerase read qualityâ=â75, minimum polymerase read lengthâ=â50). In total, 97âGb clean sequence data were obtained with an average subread length of 8.1âkb and an N50 subread length of 11.2âkb (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1b</a>).</p><p>Owing to the high error rate of SMRT reads, we constructed a PCR-free paired-end library with 500âbp insert size using the whole-genome DNA of <i>T. urartu</i> with the PCR-free protocol (Illumina kit FC-121-3001) and sequenced it by Hiseq2500 with two lanes. In total, 130âGb whole genome shotgun paired-end reads with 250âbp read length were obtained (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1b</a>). Subsequently, we filtered the low quality reads and contamination reads with bacterial genome and vectors and obtained 107âGb (21Ã) clean reads, which was used for error correction of the SMRT reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">BAC assembly</h3><p>For assembly, the pipeline flowchart outlined in Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1a</a> was followed. First, the Illumina clean reads in each BAC pool were separately assembled into contigs using MaSuRCA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. The MaSuRCA genome assembler. Bioinformatics 29, 2669â2677 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1899">36</a></sup>. Then, Illumina clean reads in each vertical BAC pool were aligned against the contigs in all horizontal BAC pools with BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1903">37</a></sup> and vice versa. The reads, which had â¥90% coverage and â¥99% identity, and appeared only once in all crossing BAC pools, were selected as input reads to the BAC at the cross point. Those input reads assigned to each BAC were assembled again using MaSuRCA software to obtain sequence contigs of each individual BAC. At this stage, the total contig length of each BAC reached 125âkb and the contig N50 was 35âkb on average.</p><p>Next, 107âGb of PCR-free Illumina clean reads with 250âbp read length was used to correct the 97âGb (19.5Ã) PacBio raw reads using Proovread<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Hackl, T., Hedrich, R., Schultz, J. &amp; FÃ¶rster, F. proovread: large-scale high-accuracy PacBio correction through iterative short read consensus. Bioinformatics 30, 3004â3011 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1910">38</a></sup>, yielding 72âGb (14Ã) of corrected PacBio reads. To fill gaps between contigs in each BAC, the corrected PacBio reads were aligned to the BAC contigs with BLASR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Chaisson, M. J. &amp; Tesler, G. Mapping single molecule sequencing reads using basic local alignment with successive refinement (BLASR): application and theory. BMC Bioinformatics 13, 238 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1914">39</a></sup> (parameters identityâ=â95%, minlengthâ=â1âkb). Subsequently, the sequence contigs of each BAC were connected with the best-aligned PacBio reads using a customized perl script. After this process, 46,374 of 47,223 BACs (98.2%) were assembled into a single contig and 610 BACs into two contigs, and more than 99.4% of BACs showed an assembled sequence length larger than 100âkb. Finally, we connected the BAC sequences iteratively into contigs based on their overlapping relationship within FPC contig, and obtained 5.33âGb in total length with a contig N50 of 183âkb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Assembly of missing regions</h3><p>Owing to the cleavage bias of restriction enzymes and low genome coverage (8Ã) of the BAC libraries used for FPC construction, some regions of the genome were missed from the BAC sequences. To assemble the missed regions, we tried to assemble the whole genome using the corrected PacBio reads with Celera Assembler. However, the Celera Assembler encountered an error during the assembly. The main reason might be the low sequencing depth of PacBio reads for such a complex genome. Therefore, we retrieved the corrected SMRT reads and the previously reported sequence contigs of <i>T. urartu</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ling, H.-Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1928">6</a></sup>, which were not covered by the BAC sequences at a minimum sequence identity of 95% and 98%, respectively, for assembly. We assembled them using MaSuRCA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the MaSuRCA mega-reads algorithm. Genome Res. 27, 787â792 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1932">40</a></sup> with default parameters. In total, we assembled additional 204âMb of sequences into contigs, and added them to the genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Scaffolding using BioNano genome map and 10âÃâgenomics linked reads</h3><p>For construction of a BioNano genome map, 10-day-old seedlings of G1812 were harvested. The DNA isolation, sequence-specific labelling of megabase gDNA for Irys mapping by nicking, labelling, repairing, and staining (NLRS) and chip analysis were performed by Genergy Bio Technology according to the manufacturerâs instructions (BioNano Genomics). In brief, the enzyme Nt.BspQI with an appropriate label density (11.5 labels per 100âkb) was selected and applied to digest long-range DNA fragments. Then, the NLRS number per DNA fragment was determined using the BioNano Irys system. In total, 502âGb BioNano mapping molecules with an average length of 265.71âkb was collected. After filtering the molecules with a cutoff at a minimum length of 150âkb and 8 labels per molecule, 417âGb BioNano molecules (83Ã effective depth) with an average length of 294.95âkb was obtained (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1b</a>). Furthermore, we used autonoise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771â776 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1947">5</a></sup> and other default parameters in IrysSolve tools based on <i>T. urartu</i> genome sequences to determine the de novo assembly noise. The RefAligner and Assembler programs in IrysSolve tools were used to assemble these BioNano molecules with initial assembly <i>P</i> value of 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup> and extension/refinement <i>P</i> value of 1âÃâ10<sup>â11</sup>. After the aforementioned processes, 9,112 BioNano genome maps with a total map length of 4.68âGb were generated. The N50 length of the BioNano genome map was 0.61âMb and the average map length was 0.54âMb.</p><p>We used BioNano genome maps and the <i>T. urartu</i> sequence contigs to generate hybrid maps with initial and final alignment <i>P</i> value of 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>, chimaeric/conflicting <i>P</i> value of 1âÃâ10<sup>â13</sup> and merging <i>P</i> value of 1âÃâ10<sup>â11</sup>. The sequence contigs that had conflicts with BioNano genome maps were cut into sub-sequences for additional hybrid map generation. We identified only 631 chimaeric contig assemblies. Finally, hybrid sequence scaffolds were generated based on these hybrid genome maps. The overlapping contigs in hybrid scaffolds were aligned using MUMmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Delcher, A. L., Phillippy, A., Carlton, J. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast algorithms for large-scale genome alignment and comparison. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 2478â2483 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1987">41</a></sup> and merged into sequence contigs.</p><p>For generation of 10Ã Genomics linked reads, the leaf DNA of G1812 was extracted from 10-day-old seedlings and used for the construction of 10Ã Genomics libraries following the manufacturerâs protocol (10Ã Genomics). Then, we used the chromium system to barcode short fragments onto long DNA molecules (â¥50âkb), and HiSeq X Ten to sequence these short fragments into standard Illumina paired-end reads as linked-reads. Short reads from the same long DNA molecule shared the same barcode. In total, 57âGb Illumina paired-end reads (11Ã effective depth) were produced. Furthermore, we used the software longranger-2.1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zheng, G. X. et al. Haplotyping germline and cancer genomes with high-throughput linked-read sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 303â311 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28976e1994">4</a></sup> to align the linked reads to the scaffolds of <i>T. urartu</i>. A mean molecule length of 31.39âkb and mean of 21 linked reads per molecule were obtained. Subsequently, the large_sv_call subprogram in longranger-2.1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zheng, G. X. et al. Haplotyping germline and cancer genomes with high-throughput linked-read sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 303â311 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2001">4</a></sup> was used to find connection information for scaffolds in the <i>T. urartu</i> genome. After this step, a large number of sequence scaffolds and contigs that were not on BAC FPC and were not connected by BioNano genome maps owing to their short length were connected to longer scaffolds (&gt;100âkb).</p><p>Additionally, the previously reported 10-kb and 20-kb mate pair reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ling, H.-Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2011">6</a></sup> were also used to connect sequence contigs into scaffolds using SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578â579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2015">42</a></sup> with default parameters. To minimize errors introduced in the scaffolding step, we only connected the sequence contigs supported by linkage evidence from BioNano and 10Ã Genomics. The SMRT raw reads were also applied to fill the gap in scaffolds using PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2019">43</a></sup> with default parameters to generate longer sequence contigs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Generation of pseudomolecules</h3><p>To anchor the assembled sequences onto chromosomes, we developed an F2 population containing 475 individuals from a cross between accessions G1812 and G3146. We sequenced this population using the restriction enzyme TaqI-associated DNA sequencing (RAD-seq) method for calling SNPs and constructing a genetic map. In total, 981âGb Illumina sequences was generated (1,028âMb per F2 individual on average). These Illumina reads were mapped to our assembled sequences using BWA MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2032">35</a></sup>, and SNPs were called using the GATK pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297â1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2036">44</a></sup>. In total, 3,751,342 SNPs were identified between G1812 and G3146. The SNPs were then filtered using parameters DP (depth) â¥2 and MAF (minor allele frequency) â¥ 0.1. Finally, 430,979 high quality SNPs were selected and used for genotyping the sequenced F2 individuals. Adjacent SNPs were merged together using a sliding window method (window:50 SNPs; step:50 SNPs) into bins. The bins as input markers were grouped into seven linkage groups through the ML (maximum likelihood) algorithm in joinMAP 4.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Stam, P. Construction of integrated genetic linkage maps by means of a new computer package: Join Map. Plant J. 3, 739â744 (1993)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2040">45</a></sup>, and the linkage map of bins was created using the Kosambi model in MSTmap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wu, Y., Bhat, P. R., Close, T. J. &amp; Lonardi, S. Efficient and accurate construction of genetic linkage maps from the minimum spanning tree of a graph. PLoS Genet. 4, e1000212 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2044">46</a></sup>. In total, 22,386 bins were anchored on the seven chromosomes of <i>T. urartu</i>. Using the SNP bin markers, 27,587 sequence contigs were anchored onto chromosomes (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM3">1</a>). We then assigned the assembled scaffolds onto their corresponding positions on the chromosome using the mapping information from SNPs, and generated seven pseudomolecules. Subsequently, adjacent bins with the same genotype within a physical distance of 100âkb were merged into larger bins to remove bins with spurious or missing genotypes. Furthermore, we set an upper limit on the physical distance between two adjacent crossovers of at least 1âMb, and allowed only at most ten recombination events on each chromosome, and changed the incompatible genotypes that caused spurious double crossover events to missing data. Finally, we recalculated the genetic distances from the recomputed recombination frequencies through the Kosambi mapping function for each linkage group, and obtained a genetic map consisting of 4,506 high-confidence bins.
