The accumulated genetic linkage distance of the genetic map was 1,444âcM (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1e</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Assembly evaluation</h3><p>To evaluate the quality of our assembly, we compared the Tu genome to 12 previously published BAC sequences (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig5">2a</a>) from the <i>T. urartu</i> G1812 genome downloaded from NCBI (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=Triticum+urartu+BAC">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=Triticum+urartu+BAC</a>) using NUCmer (parameter: -mum -mincluster 700) in MUMmer package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Delcher, A. L., Phillippy, A., Carlton, J. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast algorithms for large-scale genome alignment and comparison. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 2478â2483 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2079">41</a></sup>. Then we drew the dot plot using mummerplot in the same package with default parameters. We identified the repeat sequences using BLAST with <i>E</i> value 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup> against the TREP database (<a href="http://botserv2.uzh.ch/kelldata/trep-db/index.html">http://botserv2.uzh.ch/kelldata/trep-db/index.html</a>) and PGSB Repeat Element Database (<a href="http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/">http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/</a>) (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig5">2</a>). All of the BAC sequences were nearly completely covered by our assembled pseudomolecules with an average coverage of 98.89% and sequence identity of 99.66%. These results indicate that we have generated a high quality genome sequence of <i>T. urartu</i>.</p><p>To evaluate the per base error rate, the WGS short reads, including âWGS HiSeq PCRFree 2âÃâ250â (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig4">1b</a>) and âWGS Hiseq 2âÃâ150 (GenBank accessions SRR124016 ~ SRR124023)â, were aligned to the <i>T. urartu</i> genome with BWA MEM. After filtering out the short alignments (&lt;50âbp), we found that 98.56% of the <i>T. urartu</i> genome was covered. Meanwhile, 98.45% of reads in âWGS HiSeq PCRFree 2âÃâ250â and 98.78% of âBGI Hiseq 2âÃâ150â reads were aligned to the <i>T. urartu</i> genome. After removing the aligned reads with identity â¤98%, we used GATK to call variants from the data above and obtained 541,849 SNPs (0.011%, one per 9âkb) and 128,592 indels with a total size of 281,155âbp (0.006% base error).</p><p>To evaluate structural chimeric error rate, we compared the <i>T. urartu</i> genome contigs and the BioNano genome map by RefAligner program in IrisSolve package from BioNano Genomics (<a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/">https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/</a>), and detected 5,346 collapse/expansion regions (&gt;1âkb) using the SV detect program in the same package. These collapse/expansion regions covered 54.92âMb (1.13%) of the assembled genome (4.86âGb), with a maximum length of 124.43âkb, a minimum length of 1,012âbp, and an average length of 10.27âkb. Among them, there were 4,971 collapsed regions covering 51.26âMb (1.05% of the assembled genome 4.86âGb) with a maximum length of 124.43âkb, a minimum length of 1,018âbp and an average length of 10.31âkb, and 375 expansions covering 3.67âMb (0.07% of total assembled genome 4.86âGb) with a maximum length of 62.36âkb, a minimum length of 1,012âbp and an average length of 9.78âkb. All the data strongly indicate that the quality of our genome assembly is high and reliable.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Annotation and analysis of repetitive elements</h3><p>Repetitive sequences and transposable elements in the <i>T. urartu</i> genome were identified using a combination of ab inito and homology-based methods at both DNA and protein levels. In brief, an ab inito repeat library for <i>T. urartu</i> was predicted using LTR_FINDER v1.0.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2151">47</a></sup>, RepeatModeler (v1.0.3) with default parameters. The library was aligned to PGSB Repeat Element Database (<a href="http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/">http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/</a>) to classify the type of each repeat family. For identification of the repeats throughout the genome, RepeatMasker (v3.2.9) was applied with both the ab initio repeat databases and Repbase (<a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase">http://www.girinst.org/repbase</a>) using the WU-BLASTX search engine. Overlapping transposable elements belonging to the same repeat class were collated and combined. In addition, we annotated the tandem repeats using the software Tandem Repeats Finder (TRF, v4.04)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2170">48</a></sup>.</p><p>Solo-LTR was identified using LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2177">49</a></sup>. A customized script was implemented to identify intact LTR/Copia and LTR/Gypsy retrotransposons. The ClustalW program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Chenna, R. et al. Multiple sequence alignment with the Clustal series of programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 3497â3500 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2181">50</a></sup> was applied to align 5â² and 3â² solo-LTRs to intact LTR elements. The evolutionary distance of the two LTR sequences was estimated using the Kimura two-parameter method embedded in baseml program in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555â556 (1997)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2185">51</a></sup>. A substitution rate of 1.3âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> mutations per site per year was used to convert evolutionary distance between 5â² and 3â² solo-LTRs to insertion age of retrotransposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404â12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2191">52</a></sup>. In total, we identified 35,559 and 48,370 intact Copia and Gypsy retrotransposons, respectively. To identify solo-LTRs, we first excluded intact LTR transposable elements from the dataset of Gypsy and Copia LTR-retrotransposons, and aligned all known LTR segments to those damaged LTR-retrotransposons. The transposons that were similar (identity &gt;85%) to a known LTR segment were identified as solo-LTRs. We verified that only 2.28% of our identified LTRs overlapped with BioNano collapse/expansion regions and 1.76% overlapped with PacBio junction regions, indicating that very few LTRs were affected by misassembly of sequences.</p><p>Simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers are useful in plant genetic analysis. Therefore, we detected SSR markers within our assembled sequences using MISA software (<a href="http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html">http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html</a>). A total of 486,506 SSRs were identified. Dinucleotide was the most common repeat motif with a frequency of 35.6% (173,125 SSRs), followed by mono- (170,845, 35.1%), tri- (88,008, 18.1%), and hexa-nucleotide (7,145, 1.5%) repeat motifs. The distribution of SSRs is shown on Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig1">1i</a> and Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig6">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Annotation and analysis of non-coding RNAs</h3><p>Non-coding RNAs of <i>T. urartu</i>, including rRNAs, tRNAs, miRNAs and snoRNAs, were analysed. We used tRNAscan-s.e. (version 1.23) with eukaryote parameters to predict tRNAs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2222">53</a></sup>. The miRNA and snoRNA genes were predicted using Infernal software (version 1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335â1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2226">54</a></sup> to search the genome against the Rfam database (<a href="http://rfam.xfam.org/">http://rfam.xfam.org/</a>, release 9.1) with default parameters. The rRNA sequences were predicted using BLASTN (<i>E</i> value &lt;1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>) to align the known rRNA genes (5S, 5.8S, 18S, and 28S) of both <i>T. aestivum</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> from GenBank to the draft genome. Additionally, lncRNAs were identified with rigorous criteria: (1) transcript length must be longer than 200âbp; (2) transcript must contain no open reading frame (ORF) longer than 50 amino acids; (3) the Coding Potential Calculator (CPC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kong, L. et al. CPC: assess the protein-coding potential of transcripts using sequence features and support vector machine. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W345âW349Â (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2249">55</a></sup> was used to predict the coding potential of each transcript, and those with CPC scores &gt;0 were discarded.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>We predicted a set of genes in the <i>T. urartu</i> genome using evidence-based Gramene pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liang, C., Mao, L., Ware, D. &amp; Stein, L. Evidence-based gene predictions in plant genomes. Genome Res. 19, 1912â1923 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2264">8</a></sup> by combining protein, cDNA, EST and RNA-seq evidence. We downloaded 559,967 mRNAs (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=Triticum+urartu">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=Triticum+urartu</a>) and 1,283,261 ESTs (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucest/?term=Triticum">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucest/?term=Triticum</a>) of <i>T. urartu</i> from NCBI nucleotide database as same-species cDNAs and same-species ESTs. SwissProt proteins for plants were cleaned up by removing redundant sequences with a minimum threshold of 80% for both identity and coverage, which left us 340,312 sequences as protein evidence. The protein evidence also included 1,795 wheat proteins downloaded from GenBank. The mRNAs and ESTs of monocot species other than wheat were used as cross-species evidence; these were downloaded from NCBI and filtered to remove redundant sequences with a cutoff of 90% for both identity and coverage, which resulted in 548,604 cDNAs and 978,696 ESTs. RNA-seq data from 243 samples (2.47âTb) of <i>T. urartu</i> and bread wheat were downloaded from NCBI and were assembled into contigs using SOAPdenovo-trans v1.03 (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/SOAPdenovo-Trans.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/SOAPdenovo-Trans.html</a>). The assembled contigs were used as same-species EST evidence.</p><p>The transcripts predicted from each evidence type were combined using the Gramene pipeline to generate 115,255 potential genes as a raw set, of which the majority were spurious genes with only EST or RNA-seq evidence support. The expression value of each gene in 243 wheat RNA-seq samples was calculated using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511â515, (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2299">56</a></sup> (-u). The raw gene set was filtered to generate a core set of 41,507 protein-coding genes by removing transposable element-related genes, pseudogenes and non-coding genes. We simply define a gene to be transposable element-related if its protein has &gt;50 amino acids or &gt;50% of its protein length aligned to the annotated transposons. For pseudogenes, we treated single-exon and multi-exon genes separately. For a single-exon gene, if its protein is fully covered by a multi-exon gene, it is designated as a pseudogene. For multi-exon gene, if its protein is fully covered by another gene, and its protein length is &lt;70% of the latter, and its expression in RNA-seq data is lower than half of the average of all genes, it is designated as a pseudogene. For genes without protein evidence, a minimum cutoff of 50 amino acids was used to distinguish coding from non-coding genes. For single-exon genes with only EST or RNA-seq evidence support, we further filtered out those with protein length &lt;100 amino acids and expression level in RNA-seq data lower than half of the average of all genes.</p><p>We categorized 37,516 genes (90.4%) with multiple types of evidence support as high-confidence and 3,991 genes (9.62%) with single type of evidence support as low-confidence. The number of genes supported by each evidence type is summarized in Extended Data TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab2">1a</a>. Protein domains of each gene were annotated using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240, (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2309">57</a></sup> by searching against publicly available databases, including ProDom (<a href="http://prodom.prabi.fr/">http://prodom.prabi.fr/</a>), Prints (<a href="http://www.bioinf.manchester.ac.uk/dbbrowser/PRINTS/">http://www.bioinf.manchester.ac.uk/dbbrowser/PRINTS/</a>), Pfam (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/">http://pfam.xfam.org/</a>), Smart (<a href="http://smart.embl-heidelberg.de/">http://smart.embl-heidelberg.de/</a>), Panther (<a href="http://www.pantherdb.org/">http://www.pantherdb.org/</a>), Superfamily (<a href="http://supfam.org/SUPERFAMILY/">http://supfam.org/SUPERFAMILY/</a>), PIR (<a href="http://pir.georgetown.edu/">http://pir.georgetown.edu/</a>) and Prosite (<a href="http://prosite.expasy.org/">http://prosite.expasy.org/</a>). Overall, 73.80% of the predicted proteins were found to contain InterPro domains. In addition, 55.08% of predicted genes have been classified by gene ontology terms and 11.35% of the genes were mapped to known biological pathways. Gene function was annotated according to the best matched proteins of <i>B. distachyon</i> (<a href="http://genome.jgi.doe.gov">http://genome.jgi.doe.gov</a>), and rice (<a href="http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/downloads_gad.shtml">http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/downloads_gad.shtml</a>) using BLASTP with both minimum identity and coverage of 30% as thresholds (Extended Data TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Tab2">1c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Segmentally and tandemly duplicated genes</h3><p>To understand the chromosomal distribution of duplicated genes, we identified segmentally and tandemly duplicated genes in the Tu genome. Segmentally duplicated genes were identified in collinear segments, which contain at least two collinear gene pairs that were not separated by more than two non-collinear genes. Tandemly duplicated genes were paralogues that were located close to each other, and were not separated by more than two genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Orthologous genes between <i>T. urartu</i> and other grass genomes</h3><p>We applied the standard OrthoMCL pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2412">58</a></sup> to identify orthologous gene families among five grass species including <i>T. urartu</i>, rice, maize, sorghum and <i>B. distachyon</i>. The longest protein from each gene was selected, and the proteins with a length less than 30 amino acids were removed. After this step, pairwise sequence similarities between all input protein sequences were calculated using BLASTP with an <i>E</i> value cut-off of 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>. Markov clustering (MCL) of the resulting similarity matrix was used to define the orthologue cluster structure of the proteins, using an inflation value (â<i>I</i>) of 1.5 (OrthoMCL default). Then, comparative analysis was performed among <i>T. urartu</i>, rice, maize, sorghum and <i>B. distachyon</i> (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig7">4a</a>)</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Transcription factor analysis</h3><p>To identify transcription factors in <i>T. urartu</i> genome, we blasted the annotated genes against known plant transcriptional factors collected from the iTAK database (<a href="http://itak.feilab.net/cgi-bin/itak/index.cgi">http://itak.feilab.net/cgi-bin/itak/index.cgi</a>). We then assigned these genes to specific transcription factor families using the prediction tool iTAK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zheng, Y. et al. iTAK: A program for genome-wide prediction and classification of plant transcription factors, transcriptional regulators, and protein kinases. Mol. Plant 9, 1667â1670 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2458">14</a></sup>. In total, 1,779 genes were classified as transcription factors into 68 families. To compare the size of transcriptional factor families among different species of grasses, we collected transcriptional factors in other six cereal genomes from iTAK (<a href="http://itak.feilab.net/cgi-bin/itak/index.cgi">http://itak.feilab.net/cgi-bin/itak/index.cgi</a>) including <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Zea mays</i>, <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> and <i>T. aestivum</i>, and investigated the enrichment of genes in each transcriptional factor family in all studied speciesÂ (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM5">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Prolamin and disease resistance genes</h3><p>The wheat prolamin gene sequences, including those encoding HMW-GS, LMW-GS, Î±-, Î³-, Ï- and Î´-gliadin, were used as queries to blast against the <i>T. urartu</i> genome sequences with <i>E</i> value 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>, and matched sequences were extracted and manually annotated. Based on the annotation of whole gene set, we selected all of the NB-ARC domain genes and calculated their RPKM value based on the <i>T. urartu</i> RNA-seq data after inoculating with the powdery mildew pathogen <i>B. graminis</i> f. sp. <i>tritici</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Gene expression profiling in leaf, root and spike of <i>T. urartu</i>
                        </h3><p>To study gene expression profiles, the RNA-seq data of leaves and roots of two-month-old plants and young spikes with 10â12âcm length were used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ling, H.-Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2529">6</a></sup>. Poor quality or technical sequences in Illumina paired-end reads (read length 75âbp) were removed using Trimmomatic version 0.35 preprocessing tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2533">59</a></sup>. The qualified paired reads were then aligned against the IGDBv1.0 reference transcript sequences using Bowtie 2 version 2.2.6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2537">60</a></sup> to find all alignments of a read with no more than two mismatches. Subsequently, gene and isoform abundances were quantified from paired-end RNA-seq data using the RSEM software package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2541">61</a></sup>. Differentially expressed transcripts or genes with biological replicates were identified by running Bioconductor tools edgeR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139â140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2545">62</a></sup>, which implements a range of statistical methods based on the negative binomial distributions. The differentially expressed genes were partitioned into clusters with dominantly high expression in one of the three tissues by perl script in Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2550">63</a></sup>.</p><p>Furthermore, we identified the functional preference of the dominantly expressed genes in each organ using a perl script included in Trinotate (<a href="http://trinotate.github.io/">http://trinotate.github.io/</a>) to extract all Gene Ontology (GO) assignments from the TrEMBL/SwissProt databases, and used Bioconductor package GOseq to perform functional enrichment tests.</p><p>To monitor gene expression level along the <i>T. urartu</i> chromosomes in the three organs, we applied a window shift size of 5âMb in a customized perl script. For each pseudomolecule, gene expression distribution among three organs is shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig1">1k</a> and Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig6">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Comparison of <i>T. urartu</i> genome with BACs of <i>T. turgidum</i> and <i>T. aestivum</i>
                        </h3><p>To investigate sequence variation among different A genomes after polyploidization, we aligned the sequences of six BACs (881âkb in total) from the A subgenome of Tt and 11 BACs (1,423âkb in total) from the A subgenome of Ta to the Tu genome using NUCmer (parameter: -mum -mincluster 700) in MUMmer package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Delcher, A. L., Phillippy, A., Carlton, J. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast algorithms for large-scale genome alignment and comparison. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 2478â2483 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2594">41</a></sup>. Then we drew the dot plot using mummerplot in the same package with default parameters. We identified the repeat sequences using BLAST with <i>E</i> value 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup> against the TREP database (<a href="http://botserv2.uzh.ch/kelldata/trep-db/index.html">http://botserv2.uzh.ch/kelldata/trep-db/index.html</a>) and PGSB Repeat Element Database (<a href="http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/">http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/</a>) (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig8">5b</a>).</p><p>We also compared the TGACv1 A subgenome sequences of hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885â896 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2624">19</a></sup> to the Tu genome. All scaffolds which were located on Ta7A were compared to Tu7. We performed all-to-all alignment (-minIdentityâ=â80~99, -minScoreâ=â100, âfastMap) of Tu7 and Ta7A using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2628">37</a></sup>. The percentages of homologous segments on Tu7 and Ta7A were calculated using SOAP.COVERAGE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2632">64</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig8">5d</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Comparison of <i>T. urartu</i> genome with <i>T. aestivum</i> and <i>Ae. tauschii</i>
                        </h3><p>To investigate chromosomal structure variation between <i>T. urartu</i> and polyploid wheat, we compared the <i>T. urartu</i> genome with the three subgenomes (A, B, and D) of bread wheat (ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/release-28/plants/fasta/triticum_aestivum/) as well with the D genome of <i>Ae. tauschii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature 496, 91â95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2665">15</a></sup>. Using software MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2669">65</a></sup> with at least three syntenic genes, we identified orthologous blocks and plotted homologous proteins among wheat genomes. Highly similar proteins (coverage of protein length â¥80 and identity â¥80) were obtained using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2673">37</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig2">2a, b</a>, Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig8">5a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Collinearity of <i>T. urartu</i> versus <i>B. distachyon, O. sativa</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i>
                        </h3><p>We identified homologous proteins between <i>T. urartu</i> and the other three genomes using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2706">66</a></sup> with <i>E</i> value 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>, and scanned syntenic blocks consisted of homologous genes among the four genomes using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2715">65</a></sup> with at least three syntenic genes (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig9">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Evolution of ancient duplicated blocks in <i>T. urartu</i>
                        </h3><p>We performed intragenomic comparison. Using all-against-all blastp and MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2735">65</a></sup> with default parameters in search of collinear paralogous relationships, five obvious collinear blocks were found in <i>T. urartu</i>. These blocks were then compared to seven previously published duplicated chromosome pairs in rice (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig10">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec27">Comparisons of DNA and protein sequences between <i>T. urartu</i> chromosome 3 (Tu3) and <i>T. aestivum</i> chromosome 3B (Ta3B)</h3><p>Chromosome 3B of hexaploid wheat was completely sequenced and assembled using BAC-by-BAC sequencing strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science 345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2760">26</a></sup>. We performed complete and precise comparisons between Tu3 and Ta3B. The sequence of Ta3B was downloaded from the Ensembl website (ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/release-28/plants/fasta/triticum_aestivum/). To compare the DNA sequence of the two chromosomes, we performed all-to-all alignment (-minIdentityâ=â80, -minScoreâ=â100, âfastMap) of Tu3 and Ta3B with BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2764">37</a></sup>. The percentages of homologous segments on Tu3 and Ta3B were calculated using SOAP.COVERAGE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2768">64</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig11">8a</a>).</p><p>To compare the transposable element insertion date of Tu3 and Ta3B, we identified 5â² and 3â² solo-LTRs of Tu3 and Ta3B retrotransposons using LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2778">49</a></sup>. The evolutionary distance of the two LTR sequences was estimated by using the Kimura two-parameter method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555â556 (1997)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2782">51</a></sup>. A substitution rate of 1.3âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> mutations per site per year was used to convert evolutionary distance between 5â² and 3â² solo-LTRs to transposable element insertion dates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404â12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2788">52</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig11">8h</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec28">Collinearity between Tu3 and Ta3B</h3><p>We plotted homologous DNA sequences between Tu3 and Ta3B using NUCmer (parameter: -mum -mincluster 700) in MUMmer package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Delcher, A. L., Phillippy, A., Carlton, J. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast algorithms for large-scale genome alignment and comparison. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 2478â2483 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2803">41</a></sup>. Collinearity in both regions of 0â200-Mb and 400â700-Mb segments can be clearly observed between two chromosomes. Within 200â400-Mb segments, collinearity was not identified between Tu3 and Ta3B.</p><p>We also explored collinear relationships of homologous genes between Tu3 and Ta3B. Syntenic blocks containing at least three homologous gene pairs were identified using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2810">65</a></sup> with at least three syntenic genes (Extended Data Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#Fig11">8bâg</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec29">Identification of gene insertions and deletions on Tu3 and Ta3B</h3><p>We computationally identified gene insertions and deletions on Tu3 relative to Ta3B by combining data from <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bdistachyon_283_v2.1), rice (IRGSP1.0) and sorghum (<a href="http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html">http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html</a>). They are grass relatives of wheat that have well-sequenced and annotated genomic data.</p><p>MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2838">65</a></sup> was used to identify collinear duplicated gene blocks between species or genomes, including Tu3-to-Ta3B, Tu3-to-Bd, Tu3-to-Os, Tu3-to-Sb, Ta3B-to-Bd, Ta3B-to-Os and Ta3B-to-Sb. We collected 176 syntenic blocks between Tu3 and Ta3B, each containing more than five paired orthologues. Customized perl scripts were used to identify gene insertions and deletions occurred in the syntenic blocks between Tu3 and Ta3B via detecting whether the orthologues in Bd, Os and Sb exist. If a Ta3B gene had orthologues in at least two of Bd, Os and Sb but not on Tu3, a gene deletion was defined to have occurred on Tu3. A Tu3 insertion was defined if no collinear orthologues were found in any other investigated genomes, and the orthologues of two adjacent genes around the inserted segment existed in a collinear region of at least two of Bd, Os and Sb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec30">Analysis of <i>T. urartu</i> populations</h3><p>A total of 147Â <i>T. urartu</i> accessions, collected from Armenia, Iran, Iraq, Syria, Turkey and Lebanon, were used in this study (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM10">8</a>). Leaf samples from five uniform seedlings were used for total RNA extraction with Illumina TruSeq RNA Sample Prep Kit. With them, 147 paired-end libraries were constructed using Illumina Paired-End Sample Prep Kit, and sequenced by Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. In total, 63 billion paired-end reads in length of 100âbp were generated (6.3 Tb of sequences), with an average coverage depth of more than 25Ã for each accession. Then, adaptor sequence trimming and removal of low-quality reads were performed with the ngsShoRT algorithms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Chen, C., Khaleel, S. S., Huang, H. &amp; Wu, C. H. Software for pre-processing Illumina next-generation sequencing short read sequences. Source Code Biol. Med. 9, 8 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2860">67</a></sup>.</p><p>After removing adaptor sequences and reads with low sequence quality, TopHat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2867">68</a></sup> was used to map the paired-end reads against the reference sequence of G1812. Only paired-end reads that mapped uniquely to the genome were used for further analysis of variation calling. Duplicated reads were also filtered. The SNP calling were performed by SAMtools mpileup package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2871">69</a></sup>, and SNPs with minor allele frequency lower than 5% were excluded from further analyses.</p><p>For population structure analysis, the neighbour-joining tree was constructed using MEGA5 based on all of the SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2878">70</a></sup>. Population structure was calculated using STRUCTURE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Pritchard, J. K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945â959 (2000)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2882">71</a></sup>. The number of genetic clusters <i>K</i> was predefined as 1â10 to explore the population structure with three iterations. The run with maximum likelihood was used to assign individual genotypes into groups. The three groups uncovered using STRUCTURE corresponded well to those based on phylogenetic clustering with respect to accession composition in each group. The statistics of sequence diversity (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) and the population differentiation (<i>Ï</i> and <i>Î¸</i>) were computed using a 100-kb window in 10-kb steps with the PopGen package in BioPerl<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Wright, S. The genetical structure of populations. Ann. Eugen. 15, 323â354 (1951)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d28976e2900">72</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec31">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in the Nature ResearchÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#MOESM2">Reporting Summary</a> linked to this paper.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec32">Data availability</h3><p>Sequence data and assemblies have been deposited at BioProject under project accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA337888/">PRJNA337888</a>, Sequence Read ArchiveÂ <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRP081049">SRP081049</a> (PacBio reads <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR4010673">SRR4010673</a>â<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR4010781">SRR4010781</a> and Illumina PCR-free reads <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR4010671">SRR4010671</a>â<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR4010672">SRR4010672</a>), andÂ GenBank <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/MKGO00000000.1/">MKGO00000000</a> (pseudomolecules of <i>T. urartu</i>). The Tu genome annotation is available at MBKBase website (<a href="http://www.mbkbase.org/Tu/">http://www.mbkbase.org/Tu/</a>). BAC assemblies and the sequence reads of the population (including SNPs) have been deposited at GSA (<a href="http://gsa.big.ac.cn/">http://gsa.big.ac.cn/</a>) with the accession number PRJCA000369.</p></div></div></section>

Venn diagram illustrates shared and unique gene families (gene numbers in parentheses) among the five grass species. <b>b</b>, Gene ontology analysis of Tu-specific genes. Tu-specific InCat#, number of Tu-specific genes in the GO category; Total InCat#, number of total Tu genes in the GO category. <b>c</b>, Comparison of B3 transcription factors of Tu with <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bd), <i>O. sativa</i> (Os), <i>S. bicolor</i> (Sb), <i>Z. mays</i> (Zm), <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (Aet) and <i>T. aestivum</i> (Ta). *B3 transcription factors without identified subfamily.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 5 Comparison of Tu genome with other wheat genomes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Syntenic analysis of Tu genome with the D subgenome of Ta, and the genome of Aet. Each syntenic block contains five or more genes, with sequence similarity of 80% or more. <b>b</b>, Comparison of Tu genome with BACs of <i>T. turgidum</i> (Tt) and Ta. BAC KF282630 from chromosome 4 of the A subgenome of Tt contained two inserted fragments (blue arrows) that are composed of Copia RLC_WIS_B elements, which were not detected on the corresponding Tu4 region. BAC JQ354543 from chromosome 3 of the A subgenome of Ta lacked the Gypsy RLG_Jeli element (blue arrow), which was found on the corresponding region of Tu3. <b>c</b>, Comparison of the Tu genome with Ta7A scaffolds from TGACv1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885â896 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28976e3321">19</a></sup>. The dot plots on the top show comparison of two largest TaA scaffolds to corresponding parts of Tu7 chromosome. The diagonal lines on the dot plots show fine co-linearity. The lower part shows validation of the sequence assembly of Tu7 by BioNano maps. The Tu7 sequences were digested into in silico consensus maps, and the consensus maps corresponding to the two Ta7A scaffolds (green bar) are compared against their corresponding BioNano genome maps (blue bar). Each vertical line on the green/blue bars indicates a restriction enzyme cutting site (Nt.BspQI), and vertical lines between green bars and blue bars indicate alignments among these sites. The blue bars highlighted with red vertical line demonstrate that two BioNano genome maps (for example, 3912 and 6183) overlap with one another (they all have alignments on the overlapping region), although the two maps are not merged together owing to lack of coverage on the overlapping region. The high consistency of alignments between consensus maps and BioNano genome maps confirm the high quality of Tu genome assembly. Therefore, the insertion/deletion events in the dot plots should be sequence variations between the two A genomes from Ta and Tu, rather than assembly errors. <b>d</b>, Comparison of Tu7 with all Ta7A scaffolds from TGACv1 at nucleotide levels. *Nucleotide: minimum cutoff of DNA sequence alignments between Tu7 and Ta7A. <sup>â </sup>ML, minimum length (bp) to align.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 6 Synteny of <i>T. urartu</i> genome with other grass genomes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Intergenomic dot plots showing orthologous syntenies between Tu and three grass relatives <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bd), <i>O. sativa</i> (Os) and <i>S. bicolor</i> (Sb). The alignment of the Tu genome to the Bd, Os and Sb genomes demonstrates their highly collinear relationships and full coverage of Tu by orthologous chromosome segments from each of the three grass genomes. <b>b</b>, Data table showing the orthologous syntenies between Tu and the other genomes (Bd, Os and Sb).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 7 Intragenomic collinear regions of Tu corresponding to rice (Os) duplications.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Intragenomic and intergenomic dot plots show a clearly visible collinear region 1â² between Tu1 and Tu3 that is orthologous to the Os intragenomic collinear region 1. However, the collinearity of region 2â² between Tu1 and Tu3 is severely corrupted, but the corresponding Os intragenomic collinear region 2 was clearly visible. <b>b</b>â<b>e</b>, Similarly, Tu intragenomic collinear regions 10â², 3â², 4â² and 5â² were also clearly visible, which correspond to Os intragenomic collinear regions 10, 3, 4 and 5, respectively. However, the collinearity of Tu intragenomic regions corresponding to Os intragenomic collinear regions 11, 6, 7, 8 and 9 was disrupted. <b>f</b>, Data table showing the strong intragenomic collinear segments of Os and their corresponding regions of Tu (chromosomal positions are shown in Mb). *The corresponding seven pairs of ancient chromosomes based on the AGK structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Murat, F. et al. Evol. 6, 12â33 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28976e3408">24</a></sup> that are ancestral to the Tu and Os chromosomes listed in the right columns. The five clearly visible regions in Tu are marked in bold. **Os regions without visible corresponding collinear regions in Tu dot plots. <b>g</b>, Data table showing the number of collinear genes between ancestrally duplicated chromosome segments in rice and their corresponding gene numbers in Tu.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 8 Comparison of chromosome 3 of Tu with chromosome 3B of Ta.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Comparison of Tu3 with Ta3B at both nucleotide and protein levels. *Nucleotide: minimum cutoff of DNA sequence alignments between Tu3 and Ta3B. <sup>â </sup>ML, minimum length (bp) to align. <sup>â¡</sup>Protein: minimum cutoff of protein sequence alignments between Tu3 and Ta3B, and in reverse. <b>b</b>, Overall view of syntenic blocks between Tu3 and Ta3B. <b>c</b>, A syntenic block composed of five consecutive collinear gene pairs, showing large repeat insertions on Ta3B of &gt;100âkb. <b>d</b>, A syntenic block composed of seven consecutive collinear gene pairs. A 70-kb contraction is seen in Ta3B. <b>e</b>, A syntenic block with eight collinear genes interrupted by non-syntenic genes. <b>f</b>, Two segments of Tu3 and Ta3B show five discrete collinear gene pairs and gene expansions in Tu3. <b>g</b>, Genome expansion in one representative syntenic block from Bd2 (0.3âMb), Tu3 (4.4âMb), and Ta3B (11.2âMb). Compared with the Tu3 segment, large numbers of non-homologous genes and repeats can be observed in Ta3B, resulting in a 7-Mb expansion. <b>h</b>, Insertion dates of LTR retrotransposons on Tu3 and Ta3B. A recent retrotransposon burst at around 0.1âMa is observed on Ta3B but not on Tu3.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41586_2018_108_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 9 Population analysis.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of transcriptomic-based SNPs on the seven DNA pseudomolecules (Tu1âTu7) of <i>T. urartu</i>. The SNPs were calculated using a 1-Mb window. <b>b</b>, Boxplot comparison of altitude ranges of Tu accessions. The number of accessions in each group is indicated in parentheses. The line inside each box represents the median, the ends of each box define the 25th and 75th percentiles, and the error bars mark the 10th and 90th percentiles. Outliers are displayed as open circles. <b>c</b>, Analysis of genetic diversity and differentiation. The <i>Ï</i>, <i>Î¸</i> and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values were estimated for the three groups of Tu accessions using transcriptomic SNPs. <b>d</b>, Reaction phenotypes of Tu accessions to the wheat powdery mildew fungus Bgt. Most of the accessions in Groups I and III (96.7% and 90.6%, respectively) were susceptible to Bgt (race E09), whereas the majority of Group II accessions (92.2%) showed resistance. <b>e</b>, A total of 141 (top 1%; <i>Ï</i><sub>Group I</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>Group II</sub>â&gt;â7.7) and 143 (top 1%; <i>Ï</i><sub>Group III</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>Group II</sub>â&gt;â4.3) signals were considered to be candidate sweeps (dots above the dashed horizontal threshold line). The Tu accessions in Groups I, II and III were 30, 64 and 53, respectively. Blue arrows indicate the wall-associated receptor protein kinase gene (<i>TuWAK</i>, TuG1812G0400002796), whose haplotype variations showed strong associations with resistance or susceptibility to Bgt. <b>f</b>, Exon (box)âintron (line) structure of <i>TuWAK</i> and its two major haplotypes (Hap1 and Hap2). The positions of the three SNPs that differ between Hap1 and Hap2 are shown. Amino acid changes caused by these SNPs are also displayed. <b>g</b>, Distribution of Hap1 and Hap2 in the three groups of <i>T. urartu</i> accessions. Hap1 was the major haplotype in Group II, which was strongly associated with resistance to Bgt, while Hap2 was the main haplotype in Groups I and III, and associated with susceptibility to Bgt. In <b>d</b> and <b>g</b>, the accessions in each group were further sorted based on the response to powdery mildew infection (<b>d</b>) or the possession of <i>TuWAK</i> haplotypes (<b>g</b>), with the assorted accession numbers indicated in appropriate columns.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item"><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Extended Data Table 1 Summary of genome annotations of <i>T. urartu</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41586-018-0108-0/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec35" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec35-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec35">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec35-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
